<div id="review_664496381" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/664496381" />
    <a title="Willow " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4529669-willow"><img alt="Willow " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472180488p2/4529669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/664496381?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Willow " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Willow " href="/user/show/4529669-willow">Willow </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780736694681" href="/book/show/1261611-embers">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer664496381" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6310287561598387041">In our modern age, I think we tend to glamorize the turn-of-the-century upper classes. It’s probably because of Art Nouveau and the Gibson Girl. I always imagine men dressed in their fashionable tweed suits with stiff collars and ladies in long, lacey skirts with big flouncy hats. And there’s always some garden party somewhere with lots of flowers.<br/><br/>Funny thing though, authors who lived through this period don’t write about it that way. To them the period was filled with pretense, hypocrisy and pa</span>
  <span id="freeText6310287561598387041" style="display:none">In our modern age, I think we tend to glamorize the turn-of-the-century upper classes. It’s probably because of Art Nouveau and the Gibson Girl. I always imagine men dressed in their fashionable tweed suits with stiff collars and ladies in long, lacey skirts with big flouncy hats. And there’s always some garden party somewhere with lots of flowers.<br /><br />Funny thing though, authors who lived through this period don’t write about it that way. To them the period was filled with pretense, hypocrisy and passive aggression. Nobody talked, but everybody knew what the rules were, and they were rigid. Edith Wharton made New York sound so stultifying and oppressive, that careless Lily Bart was squashed like a bug in <i>The House of Mirth</i>. And Sándor Márai writes about characters who never speak, holding their emotions in (for decades) letting their hurt and disillusionment bleed the life out of them. It’s tragic, but also quite fascinating, because it’s so different from whom we are today. <br /><br />Today we talk about EVERYTHING. We post transgressions online for the world to see. We candidly talk about our horrible childhoods and our messy lives. We admit we drank too much last night, and we trash talk other people, politicians, the monarchy and celebrities. To think of waiting forty years to confront somebody seems absolutely batshit crazy when you can just post it on Twitter. Yet this is how people used to live their lives. <br /><br /><i>Embers</i> is about the General, a powerful and aristocratic Magyar lord during the Austro-Hungarian empire who’s been nursing a grudge for decades. He’s an old man now, still living in the past, refusing to allow modern conveniences intrude on his solitude at his ancestral, wooded estate. <br /><br />The tale is told slowly, almost too slowly probably for most modern readers (including me). The General has had a lot of time to think about what has happened and he has dwelled on it, rationalized it and philosophized about it (which also seems to be a big turn-of-the-century pastime). And at the end he finally talks about it. <br /><br />I think the story is beautifully told. The description is rich and lush. The audio book I listened to was wonderfully melodious. It’s a little slow though. I’m probably too impatient, and the ending didn't completely satisfy me. <br /><br />I give four stars. :D <br /><br /><br /><br /> <br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6310287561598387041" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_200227205" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/200227205" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/200227205?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=life-after-life">life-after-life</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2013">r-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=hungarian">hungarian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200227205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16912025542720025407">This book grew and accustomed itself to my senses as an oblong piece of grit would first irritate, then slowly become smoothly subsumed by the oyster surrounding it. The final result was just as beautiful and deceptively complex as a perfectly round pearl would be, a piece of wonderful simplicity with a surprisingly sordid history of formation. Fortunately, the world at large did not feel the need to wrest this slowly wrought jewel from its protective nest, unlike its more physically cohesive co</span>
  <span id="freeText16912025542720025407" style="display:none">This book grew and accustomed itself to my senses as an oblong piece of grit would first irritate, then slowly become smoothly subsumed by the oyster surrounding it. The final result was just as beautiful and deceptively complex as a perfectly round pearl would be, a piece of wonderful simplicity with a surprisingly sordid history of formation. Fortunately, the world at large did not feel the need to wrest this slowly wrought jewel from its protective nest, unlike its more physically cohesive counterpart.<br /><br />The writing grasped at beauty in form as often as some books grasp at plot or characters. This did not go so well at the beginning, and for the most part the imagery was a patronizing mess of shoddy similes. Constantly telling the reader how to picture something, think about something, rather than showing them. Yet another author that could have done better in cultivating their skill with metaphor. Needless to say, during the first half of the story my attention was not drawn into the story enough to pass by instances of misogyny and racism without feeling perturbed.<br /><br />However, as the book continued, so did the development of the writing. The beauty lessened its forceful display, and slipped more comfortably into subtly delighting my point of view. The truths that the author wanted to convey to the reader became less of a lesson, and more of a confession. The author even grew to gift their female characters with a complexity that consisted of more than a pretty face and relationships to men. This augmentation could have been better, but in this old world setting that had yet to be scarred by WWII, it would be a waste to depreciate the successes of the book with standards that at that time were not the norm. The past is a foreign country, after all.<br /><br />In addition to the moments of visual beauty, there are long passages of metaphysics to be found within these pages. While it is true that other authors have done it better, I had not yet run across one who have devoted as much of their mental capacities to the thought of old age, and come up with conclusions that are utterly heartbreaking in their beauty. I with my youth cannot claim to innately relate with all of these conclusions, but I do know something about the slow death that passion dies over the long passage of time, and I can say that the book captured that mental state, that whirling chaos of wasted dreams and futile existence, with a pain bordering on the exquisite.<br /><br />To reiterate, this book is a pearl. A single event filled with high emotion and murderous impulses, layered over with more than twoscore years of thought and solitude, enough for the fires of passion and youth to die and glaze over with the weight of accumulated smoothness wrought by time. There is a beauty here that cannot exist without implicit awareness of the closeness of death, as well as the unanswerable question of why this death has not yet come. The glowing gold of embers sinking into the dark of a once roaring fire, a last glimpse of the barest trace of light before the all encompassing night. They linger on, and that is all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16912025542720025407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2922441109" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2922441109" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/94602-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521304176p2/94602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2922441109?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/94602-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=20th-century-early-to-mid">20th-century-early-to-mid</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=its-the-quiet-ones">its-the-quiet-ones</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=melancholia">melancholia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=grand-opera">grand-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=worlds-lost-dead-and-dying">worlds-lost-dead-and-dying</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=examined-lives">examined-lives</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=woundedsoulsandfragileflowers">woundedsoulsandfragileflowers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=romantical">romantical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2922441109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4620775578287201896">This poor, deluded, tragic, ridiculous, tied up in knots, righteous, self-righteous, profound, profoundly scared, old, tired, poor, poor man. The most fucked up, yet painfully real therapy session I’ve ever read. I don’t understand why people are frustrated in their reviews that the guest didn’t get to talk more- that was the whole point. That was never going to happen. He never wanted to know (he desperately did, but not really). He’s spent too much time, too many words, too many years, buildin</span>
  <span id="freeText4620775578287201896" style="display:none">This poor, deluded, tragic, ridiculous, tied up in knots, righteous, self-righteous, profound, profoundly scared, old, tired, poor, poor man. The most fucked up, yet painfully real therapy session I’ve ever read. I don’t understand why people are frustrated in their reviews that the guest didn’t get to talk more- that was the whole point. That was never going to happen. He never wanted to know (he desperately did, but not really). He’s spent too much time, too many words, too many years, building his philosophy of the truth to actually want to know. It’s too much. You can’t let possible truth speak after that. This is no reckoning- it’s too late. I’ve been this guy when I wasn’t ready for my therapist to talk back yet- you talk and rant the whole hour to stop it because you don’t want to hear what might give you peace yet- or you do but can’t bear it yet- you’re too ashamed, too afraid, too in your feelings to let it go. This guy allows himself to hear one answer, one short answer he has prepared himself for no matter what, and that’s the only truth he can let in. There’s so much more to say here about the worlds that live on long past when events seem to state they should be extinct, about the author’s life and what he must have seen to write this (born in the Austria-Hungarian empire in 1900, died in 1989 in San Diego in a new world of so many kinds it’s mind blowing to think about), about prejudices and friendship- but all I can think about is this poor old man who left the therapist’s couch after the only time he allowed himself to sit on it, who will end his days with all he could handle. Which is, so heartbreakingly, not nearly enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4620775578287201896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_30909577" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30909577" />
    <a title="lisa_emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/247421-lisa-emily"><img alt="lisa_emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438704563p2/247421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30909577?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="lisa_emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="lisa_emily" href="/user/show/247421-lisa-emily">lisa_emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">dwellers and memorists</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to lisa_emily by: </span>
      Libby
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/247421-lisa-emily?shelf=fictions">fictions</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30909577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3414084092178305551">I loved this little book. It is short, and I could have finished within a few days, but I wanted to linger over it. What I loved most of about this book is its deliberation. The author wanted to work out one very small but potent aspect of a life- friendship and its death-and he took his time unfolding the details and complexities of feeling. In a way, one could say that nothing really happens in this book, except memories and thoughts. And it is true, the first half of the book consists of the </span>
  <span id="freeText3414084092178305551" style="display:none">I loved this little book. It is short, and I could have finished within a few days, but I wanted to linger over it. What I loved most of about this book is its deliberation. The author wanted to work out one very small but potent aspect of a life- friendship and its death-and he took his time unfolding the details and complexities of feeling. In a way, one could say that nothing really happens in this book, except memories and thoughts. And it is true, the first half of the book consists of the main character's (the General) memories of his childhood. The second half is built on the dinner &amp; conversation between the General and his exiled childhood friend, Konrad. The whole plot of the story can be summed up as thus: A man who is betrayed by both his wife and best friend becomes a philosopher.<br /><br />This book is exceptional is its carefully crafted ambiance and the internal musing of the main character. So much in today we are in a hurry to have things happen that we rarely reflect about our reactions or the reasons why others make the choices that they make. In this book, the General spends a lifetime reflecting upon what makes up a character and how that character unfolds in a person. When you look at someone you are closest to, can you understand what motivates that person and can you ever be objective enough to be reconciled with that person's choice, even if it opposes you? <br /><br />And then there is the language- here is an excerpt:<br /><br />"The castle was a closed world, like a great granite mausoleum full of the moldering bones of generations of men and women from earlier times, in their shrouds of slowly disintegrating gray silk or black cloth. It enclosed silence itself as if it were a prisoner persecuted for his beliefs, wasting away numbly, unshaven and in rags on a pile of musty rotting straw in a dungeon. It also enclosed memories as if they were the dead, memories that lurked in damp corners the way mushrooms, bats, rats, and beetles lurk in the mildewed cellars of old houses. Door-latches gave off the traces of a once-trembling hand, the excitement of a moment long gone, so that even now another hand hesitated to press down on them. Every house in which passion had loosened itself on people in all its fury exudes such intangible presences."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3414084092178305551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5875121839549849509" style="display:none">Come in. Come in, come in, my friend. There was even a picture of a gate on the cover of the book. It was a simple tale that raised a zen koan in its final pages: <i>What was his original face before his parents were born</i>? Ruminating on that is The Gate that gets you into The Way. I would get kicked out of zen school because I like shortcuts and glib renderings and thought about that koan just long enough to consider history as some long river that any individual changes just by dipping his toe in. Next book.<br /><br />Well, this was the <i>next</i> book. There is a gate on the cover. And there was this:<br /><br /><i>Things do not simply happen to one. ... One can also shape what happens to one. One shapes it, summons it, takes hold of the inevitable. It's the human condition. A man acts, even when he knows from the very onset that his act will be fatal. He and his fate are inseparable, they have a pact with each other that molds them both. It is not true that fate slips silently into our lives. It steps through the door that we have opened, and we invite it to enter. ...</i><br /><br />There's not one answer, Grasshopper. It's a process, and a procession.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_44297587" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44297587" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44297587?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=u-of-chicago">u-of-chicago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=mitteleuropa">mitteleuropa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=lp1990s">lp1990s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=underrated">underrated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44297587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11459604731144412587">Sandor Marai has an uncanny ability to demonstrate his ideas through things that are not said. Embers is permeated with nostalgia for the past, a past that, as in Proust, cannot be recaptured. This book is excellent not just for how well it is written and how well it is structured, but for the author's ability to demonstrate his ideas through what is not said.<br/> "My homeland no longer exists . . . Everything's come apart. My homeland was a feeling, and that feeling was mortally wounded. When that </span>
  <span id="freeText11459604731144412587" style="display:none">Sandor Marai has an uncanny ability to demonstrate his ideas through things that are not said. Embers is permeated with nostalgia for the past, a past that, as in Proust, cannot be recaptured. This book is excellent not just for how well it is written and how well it is structured, but for the author's ability to demonstrate his ideas through what is not said.<br /> "My homeland no longer exists . . . Everything's come apart. My homeland was a feeling, and that feeling was mortally wounded. When that happens the only thing to do is to go away."(p 92) This is the statement of Konrad, the childhood friend of Henrik who is the protagonist of this amazing novel. <br /> We are introduced to Henrik in the first line of the book, "In the morning, the old general spent considerable time in the wine cellars," where he is aging in his seventies just like a fine wine. With his aging come memories of a long life, of youthful friends, of love and betrayal. All this is narrated in a short novel of little more than two hundred pages. It is a story that depends more on feelings and of contrasts between Henrik and his former friend Konrad who is returning for one final confrontation. The contrasts include that of passion and reason, of the world with and without music, of the differing personalities of the south, where Konrad has spent much of his life and the north of Austria and Hungary where Henrik has remained. <br />Interlaced with their lives is the figure of Nini, a mother figure to Henrik, who is described as, "a power that surged through the house, the people in it, the walls, the objects, the way some invisible galvanic current animates Punch and the Policeman on the stage at the traveling puppet show. Sometimes people have the feeling that the house and its contents could, like ancient fabrics, fall apart at a touch and crumble to nothing if Nini were not there to hold them together with her strength."(p 11) Nini is always in the background, appearing in the first chapter and present in the last. But there is also Krisztina who would marry Henrik, but spend years estranged from her husband even as she does not leave their estate. The ghost of Krisztina hovers over Henrik throughout the novel as does the aura of death. The story is part mystery and these themes are part of the mystery along with the reason for the estrangement of Henrik and Konrad.<br /> It is Henrik who enigmatically isolates himself, yet opens the house for one last elaborate meeting with Konrad, once his friend and now his nemesis. The world of the past no longer exists except in their memory. Some people have moved on, but the past must be revisited on one last evening. It is this evening that with a mere gesture Henrik throws Krisztina's diary, unopened, "into the embers of the fire," (p 204). This action symbolizes his life, his loves, his era. It is the feeling of this era which Marai is superb in capturing. It is the heavy weight of centuries of empire that is encapsulated in his simple brushstrokes. One could compare him to Mann or perhaps Proust in his ability to explore philosophy and memory and desire, but ultimately his is a unique voice that bears reading and rereading to explore the complex relationships and meanings that are hidden within his beautiful novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11459604731144412587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6399770671666655350" style="display:none"><b>(4.5)</b> This is quite the case study into how the questioning of reason can keep a person alive until that question is answered: forty-one years, memories as if they were from yesterday, holding on, never leaving, keeping that thought, those questions betwixt a myriad of synapses firing endlessly until the time comes to explode forth with this torrent of misery, of elation, flooding; at last, he can finally die.<br /><br />Many have given this a 'one' star based on the fact that Henrik, the main character, ruminates and speculates throughout the whole book and then decides not to hear <b>the answers</b>.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_666351063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/666351063" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/108788-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442498661p2/108788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/666351063?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/108788-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer666351063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13490963009060696803">Recommended by Dawn Tripp.<br/><br/>When I was in high school, I had the privilege of having one of those English teachers that you'd feel damned lucky to run across in the pages of a book, much less in real life. Morrow Jones would shape me indelibly as a reader, a writer, a film buff, a teacher, and a person. One of the many projects we took on together was that of creating fiction in unusual forms and shapes--a t shirt, a set of fake Cliff's Notes for a nonexistent book, and a story on a möbius strip. </span>
  <span id="freeText13490963009060696803" style="display:none">Recommended by Dawn Tripp.<br /><br />When I was in high school, I had the privilege of having one of those English teachers that you'd feel damned lucky to run across in the pages of a book, much less in real life. Morrow Jones would shape me indelibly as a reader, a writer, a film buff, a teacher, and a person. One of the many projects we took on together was that of creating fiction in unusual forms and shapes--a t shirt, a set of fake Cliff's Notes for a nonexistent book, and a story on a möbius strip. It's the last of these that springs to mind as I try to wrap my mind around <i>Embers</i>, for the book itself seems to twist and coil even as one is reading it. Unlike some books which culminate in a revelation (I'm thinking here of Julian Barnes's <i>The Sense of an Ending</i> as a prominent recent example), the surprise turn of events that unfolds in this book seems almost anticlimactic, as though its very purpose is to nudge one back toward the subtler twists and turns that accompanied one every step of the way. Nabokov made a strong case that every reading is a rereading, but nah, not really, not exactly. This one, though, has no qualms about grabbing you by the collar and pretty much insisting <i> get back there, page one, prestissimo!</i> If it weren't such a flagrant crime against the environment, I'd say its pages ought to repeat themselves a second time.<br /><br />Like Barnes’s novel, <i>Embers</i> is a slow build that rises to a steady boil, a set of gestures and echoes, feints and intimations that amount to more than the mere page count would suggest. Near the opening, Márai describes how in memory, “time…fades things to the colorlessness of ancient photographs fixed on metal plates. Light and time erase the contours and distinctive shading of the faces. One has to angle the image this way and that until it catches the light in a particular way and one can make out the person whose feature have been absorbed into the blank surface of the plate…But then one day light strikes from a certain angle and one recaptures a face again.” This might be a fractal image of the novel as a whole, which reveals its characters from many angles, some of them partial and hazy, others sharp and incontrovertible. In tracing the friendship and then parting of ways between the General, Henrik, who grows up in a nearly calcifying privilege and wealth, and Konrad, the musician who is raised in abject poverty, Márai is able to explore many themes: the nature of friendship first and foremost, its delicate alchemy of likeness and dissimilarity, along with its uncertain relation to eros and sexual love. But there's much else: the distinction between facts and so-called "truth," the degree to which order can arrange one's world and life, and the anarchic, Dionysian, soul-shaking power of music. Márai treats this last in a manner that begs comparison to Tolstoy's in <i>The Kreutzer Sonata</i>--as something sufficiently dangerous as to threaten morality itself, even as it "demand[s]...that people be themselves in heart and mind." <br /><br />As for its own musicality, in Carol Brown Janeway's translation, <i>Embers</i> advances with a quieter music, not that which "required brass, and a drum major to march in front and throw his silver staff periodically into the air," nor the Chopin that nearly causes furniture to levitate at one point. I suppose the term "quiet novel" gets overused (as for mine, they all murmur and occasionally thrum, guns-ablazing thrillers barely louder than the introspective consciousness-benders). But this novel invites it in that quiet itself is so richly, melodiously captured: "The two men are speaking in hushed tones and yet their voices echo: like stringed instruments, the ancient wooden panels covering the walls also vibrate to the muffled words, amplifying them." <br /><br />It's such echoes, and of course the figurative ones even more, that make <i>Embers</i> such a prime candidate for rereading. There is no word in the English language, insofar as I know, for the initial sound that causes echoes, the sonic flint that sets them off. I'd propose "narcissus," except that that domain name's already taken in a big way. My point is that on the second go-around, everything becomes louder in this book, from the impact of Konrad's impoverished upbringing to the early hints of the complex relationship between Henrik and Nini, his nurse. It is a book that teaches one how better to listen--to it and in general--that attunes one and resounds accordingly. If I could figure out a way to publish this review on a möbius strip, I would. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13490963009060696803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1460857075" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1460857075" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1460857075?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=1001-books-list">1001-books-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-early">20th-centurylit-early</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=eastern-central-european-lit">eastern-central-european-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1460857075" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2564198343902338558">For a long time in our lives, we knew people back in our younger days, and then lost track of them for one reason or another, occasionally thinking "I wonder whatever happened to...". There was not always a way of knowing whatever happened to some people, especially if you moved away from home and didn't have any mutual friends any more.<br/><br/>But then Facebook happened. And the world rejoiced because we could suddenly become "friends" with people we knew a gazillion years ago, see that their lives are</span>
  <span id="freeText2564198343902338558" style="display:none">For a long time in our lives, we knew people back in our younger days, and then lost track of them for one reason or another, occasionally thinking "I wonder whatever happened to...". There was not always a way of knowing whatever happened to some people, especially if you moved away from home and didn't have any mutual friends any more.<br /><br />But then Facebook happened. And the world rejoiced because we could suddenly become "friends" with people we knew a gazillion years ago, see that their lives are awesome (or suck) and that they are more awesome than we remember (or suck more than we remember). Sometimes they have different religious or political beliefs and occasionally we think "Wow, I can't believe they just said that."<br /><br />This book is from that time before Facebook.<br /><br />That's right, children - there used to be a world before Facebook. People would have to meet in person and have actual conversations. With their mouths. Face-to-face. It was crazy back then.<br /><br />Written in 1942 by a Hungarian man (I say that because I always thought the author was female though I have no idea why I thought that), it's a short-and-sweet novel about an elderly general who has an old buddy over for dinner. It's been some 40+ years since they've seen each other, their friendship ending all those years ago. And as they share their time together now, the story of their friendship (and their bromance breakup) is slowly unfolded to us, the reader.<br /><br />And, of course, there was a woman at the root of all the problems. Because that's what we do, as women. We cause all the problems.<br /><br />The story is actually rather pretty. There are lovely phrases, rather philosophic in parts, really kind of smart.<br /><br />But I didn't love it.<br /><br />I know, I know, what the fuck, right. It has all the right components, but I felt that in the end it's just not a story that will stick with me the way it will for a lot of other readers.<br /><br />It's a slow novel, a short novel, and it reads quickly. It's one of those books that I would sort of consider a "quiet novel", in that there's not a whole lot going on, but what <i>is</i> going on is rather incisive. But unlike other "quiet novels" that I have loved, this one failed to wrap me in its arms like a warm blanket.<br /><br />Worth a read, however, and it won't take long for you to read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2564198343902338558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1083525165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1414210614956710113">A man has received a note from another man he hasn't seen in 41 years 43 days. As he knows the precise date of their last meeting, it must have been significant. He instructs Nini, the woman who fed him from birth, to prepare the formal dining room and other rooms for the man's guest. These are rooms that have not been used by him since that long ago day.<br/><br/>Marai manages to create and maintain tension throughout. This is relatively short, fortunately, for we are given no relief. The prose is wonder</span>
  <span id="freeText1414210614956710113" style="display:none">A man has received a note from another man he hasn't seen in 41 years 43 days. As he knows the precise date of their last meeting, it must have been significant. He instructs Nini, the woman who fed him from birth, to prepare the formal dining room and other rooms for the man's guest. These are rooms that have not been used by him since that long ago day.<br /><br />Marai manages to create and maintain tension throughout. This is relatively short, fortunately, for we are given no relief. The prose is wonderful. After the guest arrives, the rest of the novel is almost entirely dialogue. I think some have complained that the novel is primarily a monologue. While not 100% true, yes, the important part of the novel is spoken by the host. I did not find this a negative. Marai could have told this story in the third person, but it would not have been as powerful. I think also it would have lacked the revealing of character that this monologue provided.<br /><br />I have said elsewhere that I have a fictitious top ten list. Pressed, I'm not sure I could get past the top two or three, the remainder of such a list might be fluid, depending on the day you ask. I am sure this book belongs on my list, and maybe among those that have a permanent place. I will definitely be looking for another by this author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1414210614956710113" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9Oi4pEmaX3LFSMg1ykx+WDZWBAgkmQVAfec0QZzl6VhCTlKq6K/JbvxV/S5cV8Ix8S0ER3dcRAN8blZX00k9Ng==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('etJLknAnAXEE3jfnU/NGMy7KmXbv+YIws1aQPz0o9UDMdKGc0RKXbT3DAvzF6Ppa6bGZObw8w3Oy3/IpcoQhLg==')})
  }
//]]>

Herida en su orgullo por la prohibición materna de asistir al ágape, Antoinette, de catorce años, observa con amargura los agitados preparativos del baile y siente que ha llegado la ocasión de enfrentarse a su madre, afirmarse a sí misma y realizar su propia entrada en la edad adulta.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4771489.S_ndor_M_rai">About Sándor Márai</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4771489.S_ndor_M_rai"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1588857344p3/4771489.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4771489.S_ndor_M_rai">Sándor Márai
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
711
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7844879477993528875">Sándor Márai (originally Sándor Károly Henrik Grosschmied de Mára) was a Hungarian writer and journalist.<br/>He was born in the city of Kassa in Austria-Hungary (now Košice in Slovakia) to an old family of Saxon origin who had mixed with magyars through the centuries. Through his father he was a relative of the Ország-family. In his early years, Márai travelled to and lived in Frankfurt, Berlin, and P</span>
  <span id="freeText7844879477993528875" style="display:none">Sándor Márai (originally Sándor Károly Henrik Grosschmied de Mára) was a Hungarian writer and journalist.<br />He was born in the city of Kassa in Austria-Hungary (now Košice in Slovakia) to an old family of Saxon origin who had mixed with magyars through the centuries. Through his father he was a relative of the Ország-family. In his early years, Márai travelled to and lived in Frankfurt, Berlin, and Paris and briefly considered writing in German, but eventually chose his mother language, Hungarian, for his writings. He settled in Krisztinaváros, Budapest, in 1928. In the 1930s, he gained prominence with a precise and clear realist style. He was the first person to write reviews of the work of Kafka.<br />He wrote very enthusiastically about the Vienna Awards, in which Germany forced Czechoslovakia and Romania to give back part of the territories which Hungary lost in the Treaty of Trianon. Nevertheless, Márai was highly critical of the Nazis as such and was considered "profoundly antifascist," a dangerous position to take in wartime Hungary.<br />Marai authored forty-six books, mostly novels, and was considered by literary critics to be one of Hungary's most influential representatives of middle class literature between the two world wars. His 1942 book Embers (Hungarian title: A gyertyák csonkig égnek, meaning "The Candles Burn Down to the Stump") expresses a nostalgia for the bygone multi-ethnic, multicultural society of the Austro-Hungarian Empire, reminiscent of the works of Joseph Roth. In 2006 an adaptation of this novel for the stage, written by Christopher Hampton, was performed in London.<br />He also disliked the Communist regime that seized power after World War II, and left – or was driven away – in 1948. After living for some time in Italy, Márai settled in the city of San Diego, California, in the United States.<br />He continued to write in his native language, but was not published in English until the mid-1990s. Márai's Memoir of Hungary (1944-1948) provides an interesting glimpse of post World War II Hungary under Soviet occupation. Like other memoirs by Hungarian writers and statesmen, it was first published in the West, because it could not be published in the Hungary of the post-1956 Kádár era. The English version of the memoir was published posthumously in 1996. After his wife died, Márai retreated more and more into isolation. He committed suicide by a gunshot to his head in San Diego in 1989.<br />Largely forgotten outside of Hungary, his work (consisting of poems, novels, and diaries) has only been recently "rediscovered" and republished in French (starting in 1992), Polish, Catalan, Italian, English, German, Spanish, Portuguese, Czech, Danish, Icelandic, Korean, Dutch, and other languages too, and is now considered to be part of the European Twentieth Century literary canon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7844879477993528875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1250451-a-gyerty-k-csonkig-gnek">Quotes from Embers</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;No, the secret is that there's no reward and we have to endure our characters and our natures as best we can, because no amount of experience or insight is going to rectify our deficiencies, our self-regard, or our cupidity. We have to learn that our desires do not find any real echo in the world. We have to accept that the people we love do not love us, or not in the way we hope. We have to accept betrayal and disloyalty, and, hardest of all, that someone is finer than we are in character or intelligence.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/104477-no-the-secret-is-that-there-s-no-reward-and-we">106 likes</a>

As I said, later I understood that someone who flees into honesty like that fears something, fears that her life will fill with something that can no longer be shared, a genuine secret, indescribable, unutterable.&rdquo;</span>
